[tr-stream call-0005:z-ai/glm-5.2] I need to think deeply about this cipher and possible attacks. Let me reason through the structure carefully and decide on the best approach.

The cipher:
- Initial: `(L0, R0) = (L, R^L)` from plaintext `(L,R)`
- Round i (0..3): `(L_{i+1}, R_{i+1}) = (R_i, L_i ^ F(R_i ^ K_i))`
- Final: after 4 rounds, post-mix `L_4 ^= R_4`, output `(R_4, L_4)`

So from ciphertext `C`, we get `(R_4, L_4')` where `L_4' = L_4 ^ R_4`, i.e. we can recover `L_4 = L_4' ^ R_4`.

So we know both `L_4` and `R_4` for each pair.

Feistel inversion gives us round-by-round:
- From `(L_{i+1}, R_{i+1})`: `R_i = L_{i+1}`, `L_i = R_{i+1} ^ F(R_i ^ K_i)`

So if we knew K3, we could invert round 3 to get `(L3, R3)` for all pairs. Then if we knew K2, invert to get `(L2,R2)`, etc.

The key insight: we need to find the 4 round keys (each 20-bit seed → 32-bit expanded). 

A standard approach for FEAL linear attacks: use linear approximations of the F function to recover round keys bit-by-bit. But maybe there's a simpler approach given the small key space.

Let me think about meet-in-the-middle. We have `K0, K1, K2, K3` each 20 bits. From the plaintext side, encrypting 2 rounds requires K0, K1 (40 bits). From the ciphertext side, decrypting 2 rounds requires K2, K3 (40 bits). After 2 rounds we'd meet at `(L2, R2)`. So MITM over 2^40 on each side — still way too much.

But maybe we can use a staged approach. The F function only has 8-bit-ish diffusion per byte, and the key is XORed before F. Let me think about whether a linear attack can recover keys one round at a time.

Actually, let me reconsider. The expand function maps 20 bits to 32 bits. But not all 32-bit values are reachable. Maybe there are properties of the expansion we can exploit — but the main point is still that each key is 20 bits.

Let me think about a different approach. With 32 known pairs, maybe the intended approach is a linear attack exploiting biases in the F function. But implementing a full linear attack on FEAL is complex. Let me think if there's a shortcut.

Actually, let me reconsider the feasibility of brute force. Each individual key seed is 20 bits. Can we brute force key3 (2^20) by checking consistency of some relation? When we decrypt one round from the ciphertext, we get `(L3, R3)`. But we don't have a direct check on whether `(L3, R3)` is correct without knowing the other keys.

Wait — but we DO know the plaintext. So we also know `(L0, R0)`. If we could check something about `(L3, R3)` vs `(L0, R0)` without knowing K0-K2... that would require knowing 3 rounds of encryption, which uses K0, K1, K2.

What about a linear attack? The F function in FEAL has known linear biases. The key idea is to find linear relations between input and output bits of F that hold with some probability, and use them to relate known plaintext bits to known ciphertext bits, eliminating intermediate state bits.

This is quite involved. Let me think about whether there's a simpler structural attack.

Actually, let me look more carefully at the F function and the G function for linearity. The G function is `G(a,b,x) = rot2(a+b+x)`. This is almost linear over GF(2) — addition mod 256 with a constant `x` is affine (linear + constant). The rotation is linear. So `G(a,b,x)` is an affine function of `a` and `b` over GF(2), with the constant depending on `x` and carries.

Actually, addition mod 256 is NOT linear over GF(2) due to carries. But the carry propagation might give us some linear approximations with good probability.

Let me think about this differently — maybe I should just try a more practical approach. 

With 32 pairs, the key is deterministic. Let me think about optimizing a brute-force or staged search.

One idea: brute-force K0 and K1 (2^40) is too much. But maybe we can brute-force K0 alone (2^20) and use a consistency check. After choosing K0, we can compute `(L1, R1)` for all pairs. Then we need to encrypt 3 more rounds and match ciphertext — still 3 unknown keys.

Alternatively, brute-force K3 (2^20), decrypt one round to get `(L3, R3)`. Then we need to match `(L3,R3)` computed from plaintext through 3 rounds with K0,K1,K2. Still 3 unknown keys.

What about simultaneous brute-force of just one key using linear constraints? The hint says "linear attack that recovers round-keys" and "20-bit seed makes it easier." So the intended attack is definitely a linear cryptanalysis.

Let me think about the standard FEAL-4 linear attack. In FEAL, the F function has the linear property that certain XOR combinations of output bytes relate to input bytes through the G function. Let me analyze the specific F function.

Let me look at the F function more carefully:

```
y2 = G(x3^x2, x1^x0, 1)
y3 = G(x3, y2, 0)
y1 = G(x1^x0, y2, 0)
y0 = G(x0, y1, 1)
```

OK let me think about this problem from the linear cryptanalysis angle more seriously.

In linear cryptanalysis of FEAL-4, the attack works as follows:
1. Find linear approximations of the F function that hold with non-trivial bias.
2. Use these to derive equations involving plaintext bits, ciphertext bits, and key bits.
3. Collect enough known plaintext-ciphertext pairs to evaluate the biases and determine key bits.

For the F function, a key property is that addition mod 256 with carry has linear approximations. Specifically, for byte addition `S = (a + b) mod 256`, the XOR of the MSBs satisfies:
`msb(S) = msb(a) ^ msb(b) ^ carry_in_7`

The carry into bit 7 depends on the lower 7 bits, but if we sum over many pairs and average, we get a bias.

The rotation `rot2(S)` shifts bits, so the MSB of S becomes bit 5 of the output, etc.

Actually, let me reconsider. For the G function:
`G(a, b, x) = rot2((a + b + x) & 0xFF)`

Consider the sum over GF(2) (XOR) of specific bits. The addition `t = (a + b + x) & 0xFF` is arithmetic addition. The rotation `rot2(t)` maps bit i to bit (i+2) mod 8.

Now, the key linear approximation for addition modulo 2^n: Bit i of `(a + b)` (over integers) equals bit i of `a XOR b` XOR the carry from bit i-1. The carry propagates. But the MSB (bit 7) of `(a+b)`: `bit7(a+b) = bit7(a) XOR bit7(b) XOR carry_7`, where carry_7 depends on bits 0-6 of a and b. When averaging over random inputs, P(carry_7 = 0) ≈ 1/2 + some bias depending on the distribution. Actually, for truly random a and b, the carry distribution converges to a specific distribution, but the bias of `bit7(a+b) XOR bit7(a) XOR bit7(b)` is not great.

However, in FEAL attacks, what matters is that for certain bit combinations, the bias is high enough. Let me think about which bits have the best bias.

For addition of two 8-bit numbers, the probability that `bit_i(a+b) = bit_i(a) XOR bit_i(b)` (i.e., carry into bit i is 0) is `(1/2) + (1/2)^{i}` for random inputs (for bit i counting from 0, i.e., the (i+1)th bit). Wait, that's for independent random bits. For bit 7 (MSB), P(carry_7 = 0) = 1/2 + 1/2^8 ≈ 0.5039. That's a very small bias (about 0.0039).

Then for a single pair, K1 = R1_0 ^ F^{-1}(R2_0 ^ R0_0). Check if K1 is in the valid key set. If not, skip. If yes, compute K2 for the same pair and verify across all 32 pairs.

But this is still 2^20 × 2^20 = 2^40 in the worst case.

Hmm, but the F^{-1}(R2 ^ R0) computation for a single pair 0 gives a fixed value (given K3). Let's call it T = F^{-1}(R2_0 ^ R0_0). Then K1 = R1_0 ^ T. Since R1_0 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0), we have K1 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0) ^ T.

For K1 to be valid: K1 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0) ^ T must equal expand(seed1) for some 20-bit seed1.

So for each K0, we compute K1 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0) ^ T and check if it's a valid expanded key. If F^{-1} is bijective, we just compute K1 and check membership. This still requires trying each K0 (2^20) for each K3 (2^20), giving 2^40.

Unless we can flip the structure: enumerate K0 and K1 together more efficiently.

For pair 0, the constraint is: F(R0_0 ^ K0) ^ F^{-1}(R2_0 ^ R0_0) ^ L0_0 = K1. If K1 must be a valid key, and we need R2_0 which depends on K3:

R2_0 = L3_0 = getl(C_0) ^ F(R3_0 ^ K3), where R3_0 = getr(C_0) ^ getl(C_0).

So T (= F^{-1}(R2_0 ^ R0_0) = F^{-1}(L3_0 ^ R0_0)) depends on K3, but F^{-1}(L3_0 ^ R0_0) = F^{-1}(getl(C_0) ^ F(R3_0 ^ K3) ^ R0_0).

This is getting complicated. Let me think about whether there's a more efficient approach.

Actually, let me reconsider. Maybe I should just check whether F is a bijection, and if so, use a different 2-round splitting that requires only 2^20 computations.

Alternative idea: Can we isolate K0 with a 2^20 brute-force using 2 pairs?

From the plaintext side after 1 round with K0:
- L1 = R0 (known from plaintext), R1 = L0 ^ F(R0 ^ K0) (K0 unknown)
- After 2 rounds with K0, K1:
  - L2 = R1, R2 = L1 ^ F(R1 ^ K1) = R0 ^ F(R1 ^ K1)

From the ciphertext side after decrypting 1 round with K3:
- L3 = R2 (decrypt direction), R3 = L2 ^ F(R2 ^ K2) = R1 ^ F(R2 ^ K2)
- R4 = L3 ^ F(R3 ^ K3) = R2 ^ F(R3 ^ K3)
- So R2 = R4 ^ F(R3 ^ K3) = getl(C) ^ F(getr(C)^getl(C) ^ K3) = L3 (as derived above)

Actually, let me try the following approach: for the round-key recovery, split the cipher into a 2-round forward and 2-round backward computation, but with a twist.

2-round forward (K0, K1):
- (L0, R0) → (L2, R2) where L2 = L0 ^ F(R0 ^ K0), R2 = R0 ^ F(L2 ^ K1).

2-round backward (K3, K2):
- (L4, R4) → (L2, R2) where we decrypt from ciphertext.

After choosing K3, from ciphertext we get (L3, R3) = (R2, R1 ^ F(R2 ^ K2)).
After choosing K0, from plaintext we get (L1, R1).
We need L3 = L3 from ciphertext = R2 to match R2 from forward = R0 ^ F(L2 ^ K1) = R0 ^ F(L1 ^ K1) where L2 = L1 = R0, no wait.

Hmm I was computing wrong. Let me state it in terms of L1, R1, L2, R2.

Forward: (L0, R0) → (L1, R1) = (R0, L0 ^ F(R0 ^ K0)) → (L2, R2) = (R1, L1 ^ F(R1 ^ K1)) = (L0 ^ F(R0^K0), R0 ^ F(L0^F(R0^K0) ^ K1)).

Backward from ciphertext: (L4, R4) → decrypt last round → (L3, R3) = (R4 ^ F(getr(C)^getl(C) ^ K3), getr(C) ^ getl(C)).

Hmm, I realize the issue is any full splitting involves at least 2 keys on one side and 2 on the other.

But here's an idea: use the fact that K1 is determined by K0 and K3 via a constraint. From the first pair, given K0 and K3, K1 is determined. K2 is also determined by K0, K1, K3. So the total search is 2^20 (K0) × 2^20 (K3) = 2^40.

But maybe we can reduce further using multiple pairs and the constraint that K1 must be valid (20-bit expanded). For a given K0, F(R0_i ^ K0) is computed for all pairs. K1 = expand(seed1) is one of 2^20 values. The condition is R0_i ^ F((L0_i ^ F(R0_i ^ K0)) ^ K1) = L3_i for all pairs. So if we precompute L3_i for a given K3, then for a given K0, we need K1 such that F((R1_i) ^ K1) = L3_i ^ R0_i for all i.

Wait, R2 = R0 ^ F(R1 ^ K1), and L3 = R2, so L3 ^ R0 = F(R1 ^ K1). 

So for a given K3 (which gives L3_i), and a given K0 (which gives R1_i):
F(R1_i ^ K1) = L3_i ^ R0_i for all i.

If we use pair 0: K1_candidate_set_i = { K1 : F(R1_i ^ K1) = L3_i ^ R0_i }. If F is a bijection, this set has exactly one element: K1 = R1_i ^ F^{-1}(L3_i ^ R0_i). So for each (K0, K3), there's at most one K1 (from pair 0). We verify it against all other pairs.

This means the search is 2^40 but the inner work is minimal (just checking membership and verification). In C with optimization, we could potentially check each (K0, K3) combination's first pair very quickly. But 2^40 is about 10^12 iterations — at 10^9 ops/sec, that's 10^3 seconds ≈ 17 minutes. In C it might be feasible if the inner loop is tight enough.

Actually, with a small optimization: we don't need K0 × K3. For pair 0, T = F^{-1}(L3_0 ^ R0_0) depends on K3. K1 = R1_0 ^ T. R1_0 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0). So K1 = L0_0 ^ F(R0_0 ^ K0) ^ T. Since K1 must be a valid 20-bit expanded key, we can check this quickly.

But we still need to iterate over all (K0, K3) pairs. That's 2^40.

Hmm. Let me think if we can further reduce. Maybe instead of iterating over both K0 and K3, we can iterate over K0 only and use a linear check to directly determine K3.

Given K0, and using 2 pairs (pair 0 and pair j):
- For pair 0: K1 = R1_0 ^ F^{-1}(L3_0 ^ R0_0), depends on K3.
- For pair j: K1 should be the same.

But K1 depends on K3 through L3 (since L3 is a function of K3). We can't simplify easily.
